def has_respawned():\n15:     return hasattr(sys.modules['__main__'], '_respawned')\n16: \n17: \n18: def respawn_module(interpreter_path):\n19:     \"\"\"\n20:     Respawn the currently-running Ansible Python module under the specified Python interpreter.\n21: \n22:     Ansible modules that require libraries that are typically available only under well-known interpreters\n23:     (eg, ``yum``, ``apt``, ``dnf``) can use bespoke logic to determine the libraries they need are not\n24:     available, then call `respawn_module` to re-execute the current module under a different interpreter\n25:     and exit the current process when the new subprocess has completed. The respawned process inherits only\n26:     stdout/stderr from the current process.\n27: \n28:     Only a single respawn is allowed. ``respawn_module`` will fail on nested respawns. Modules are encouraged\n29:     to call `has_respawned()` to defensively guide behavior before calling ``respawn_module``, and to ensure\n30:     that the target interpreter exists, as ``respawn_module`` will not fail gracefully.\n31: \n32:     :arg interpreter_path: path to a Python interpreter to respawn the current module\n33:     \"\"\"\n34: \n35:     if has_respawned():\n36:         raise Exception('module has already been respawned')\n37: \n38:     # FUTURE: we need a safe way to log that a respawn has occurred for forensic/debug purposes\n39:     payload = _create_payload()\n40:     stdin_read, stdin_write = os.pipe()\n41:     os.write(stdin_write, to_bytes(payload))\n42:     os.close(stdin_write)\n43:     rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '--'], stdin=stdin_read)\n44:     sys.exit(rc)  # pylint: disable=ansible-bad-function\n45: \n46: \n47: def probe_interpreters_for_module(interpreter_paths, module_name):\n48:     \"\"\"\n49:     Probes a supplied list of Python interpreters, returning the first one capable of\n50:     importing the named module. This is useful when attempting to locate a \"system\n51:     Python\" where OS-packaged utility modules are located.\n52: \n53:     :arg interpreter_paths: iterable of paths to Python interpreters. The paths will be probed\n54:     in order, and the first path that exists and can successfully import the named module will\n55:     be returned (or ``None`` if probing fails for all supplied paths).\n56:     :arg module_name: fully-qualified Python module name to probe for (eg, ``selinux``)\n57:     \"\"\"\n58:     for interpreter_path in interpreter_paths:\n59:         if not == 0:\n64:                 return interpreter_path\n65:         except Exception:\n66:             continue\n67: \n68:     return None\n69: \n70: \n71: def _create_payload():\n72:     from ansible.module_utils.basic._ANSIBLE_ARGS (not launched by AnsiballZ?)')\n76:     module_fqn = sys.modules['__main__']._module_fqn\n77:     modlib_path = sys.modules['__main__']._modlib_path\n78: {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_0199ZXwAZrhRdWZ4zHmqPgPm","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Copyright: (c) 2021, Ansible Project\n     2→# GNU General Public License v3.0+ (see COPYING or https://www.gnu.org/licenses/gpl-3.0.txt)\n     3→\n     4→from __future__ import (absolute_import, division, print_function)\n     5→__metaclass__ = type\n     6→\n     7→import os\n     8→import subprocess\n     9→import sys\n    10→\n    11→from ansible.module_utils.common.text.converters import to_bytes, to_native\n    12→\n    13→\n    14→def has_respawned():\n    15→    return hasattr(sys.modules['__main__'], '_respawned')\n    16→\n    17→\n    18→def respawn_module(interpreter_path):\n    19→    \"\"\"\n    20→    Respawn the currently-running Ansible Python module under the specified Python interpreter.\n    21→\n    22→    Ansible modules that require libraries that are typically available only under well-known interpreters\n    23→    (eg, ``yum``, ``apt``, ``dnf``) can use bespoke logic to determine the libraries they need are not\n    24→    available, then call `respawn_module` to re-execute the current module under a different interpreter\n    25→    and exit the current process when the new subprocess has completed. The respawned process inherits only\n    26→    stdout/stderr from the current process.\n    27→\n    28→    Only a single respawn is allowed. ``respawn_module`` will fail on nested respawns. Modules are encouraged\n    29→    to call `has_respawned()` to defensively guide behavior before calling ``respawn_module``, and to ensure\n    30→    that the target interpreter exists, as ``respawn_module`` will not fail gracefully.\n    31→\n    32→    :arg interpreter_path: path to a Python interpreter to respawn the current module\n    33→    \"\"\"\n    34→\n    35→    if has_respawned():\n    36→        raise Exception('module has already been respawned')\n    37→\n    38→    # FUTURE: we need a safe way to log that a respawn has occurred for forensic/debug purposes\n    39→    payload = _create_payload()\n    40→    stdin_read, stdin_write = os.pipe()\n    41→    os.write(stdin_write, to_bytes(payload))\n    42→    os.close(stdin_write)\n    43→    rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '--'], stdin=stdin_read)\n    44→    sys.exit(rc)  # pylint: disable=ansible-bad-function\n    45→\n    46→\n    47→def probe_interpreters_for_module(interpreter_paths, module_name):\n    48→    \"\"\"\n    49→    Probes a supplied list of Python interpreters, returning the first one capable of\n    50→    importing the named module. The paths will be probed\n    54→    in order, and the first path that exists and can successfully import the named module will\n    55→    be returned (or ``None`` if probing fails for all supplied paths).\n    56→    :arg module_name: fully-qualified Python module name to probe for (eg, ``selinux``)\n    57→    \"\"\"\n    58→    for interpreter_path in interpreter_paths:\n    59→        if not os.path.exists(interpreter_path):\n    60→            continue\n    61→        try:\n    62→            rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '-c', 'import {0}'.format(module_name)])\n    63→            if rc == 0:\n    64→                return interpreter_path\n    65→        except Exception:\n    66→            continue\n    67→\n    68→    return None\n    69→\n    70→\n    71→def _create_payload():\n    72→    from ansible.module_utils import basic\n    73→    smuggled_args = getattr(basic, '_ANSIBLE_ARGS')\n    74→    if not smuggled_args:\n    75→        raise Modules are encouraged\n    to call `has_respawned()` to defensively guide behavior before calling ``respawn_module``, and to ensure\n    that the target interpreter exists, as ``respawn_module`` will not fail gracefully.\n\n    :arg interpreter_path: path to a Python interpreter to respawn the current module\n    \"\"\"\n\n    if has_respawned():\n        raise Exception('module has already been respawned')\n\n    # FUTURE: we need a safe way to log that a respawn has occurred for forensic/debug purposes\n    payload = _create_payload()\n    stdin_read, stdin_write = os.pipe()\n    os.write(stdin_write, to_bytes(payload))\n    os.close(stdin_write)\n    rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '--'], stdin=stdin_read)\n    sys.exit(rc)  # pylint: disable=ansible-bad-function\n\n\ndef probe_interpreters_for_module(interpreter_paths, module_name):\n    \"\"\"\n    Probes a supplied list of Python interpreters, returning the first one capable of\n    importing the named module. This is useful when attempting to locate a \"system\n    Python\" where OS-packaged utility modules are located.\n\n    :arg interpreter_paths: iterable of paths to Python interpreters. The paths will be probed\n    in order, and the first path that exists and can successfully import the named module will\n    be returned (or ``None`` if probing fails for all supplied paths).\n    :arg module_name: fully-qualified Python module name to probe for (eg, ``selinux``)\n    \"\"\"\n    for interpreter_path in interpreter_paths:\n        if not os.path.exists(interpreter_path):\n            continue\n        try:\n            rc = subprocess.call([interpreter_path, '-c', 'import {0}'.format(module_name)])\n            if rc == 0:\n                return interpreter_path\n        except Exception:\n            continue\n\n    return None\n\n\ndef _create_payload():\n    from ansible.module_utils import basic\n    smuggled_args = getattr(basic, '_ANSIBLE_ARGS')\n    if not smuggled_args:\n        raise Exception('unable to access ansible.module_utils.basic._ANSIBLE_ARGS (not launched by AnsiballZ?)')\n    module_fqn = sys.modules['__main__']._module_fqn\n    modlib_path = sys.modules['__main__']._modlib_path\n    respawn_code_template = '''\nimport runpy\nimport sys\n\nmodule_fqn = '{module_fqn}'\nmodlib_path = '{modlib_path}'\nsmuggled_args = b\"\"\"{smuggled_args}\"\"\".strip()\n\n\nif __name__ == '__main__':\n    sys.path.insert(0, modlib_path)\n\n    from ansible.module_utils import basic\n    basic._ANSIBLE_ARGS = smuggled_args\n\n    runpy.run_module(module_fqn, init_globals=dict(_respawned=True), run_name='__main__', alter_sys=True)\n    '''\n\n    respawn_code = respawn_code_template.format(module_fqn=module_fqn, modlib_path=modlib_path, smuggled_args=to_native(smuggled_args))\n\n    return respawn_code","is_error":false}]},"parent_tool_use_id":"toolu_01KJMhY69BmWPZJ4naaPzz5S","session_id":"9bf8276d-2d55-4384-8119-ff65a5b254be","uuid":"3ec2e0ee-b6cc-4f76-bacc-e66531780ec7"}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01T4PNY7hnbEWKHmJf2yXko1","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: # -*- coding: utf-8 -*-\\n2: # Copyright (c) 2019 Ansible Project\\n3: # (c) 2016 Toshio Kuratomi <tkuratomi@ansible.com>\\n4: # Simplified BSD License (see licenses/simplified_bsd.txt or https://opensource.org/licenses/BSD-2-Clause)\\n5: \\n6: from __future__ import absolute_import, division, print_function\\n7: __metaclass__ = type\\n8: \\n9: import codecs\\n10: import datetime\\n11: import json\\n12: \\n13: from ansible.module_utils.common._collections_compat import Set\\n14: from ansible.module_utils.six import (\\n15:     PY3,\\n16:     binary_type,\\n17:     iteritems,\\n18:     text_type,\\n19: )\\n20: \\n21: try:\\n22:     codecs.lookup_error('surrogateescape')\\n23:     HAS_SURROGATEESCAPE = True\\n24: except LookupError:\\n25:     HAS_SURROGATEESCAPE = False\\n26: \\n27: \\n28: _COMPOSED_ERROR_HANDLERS = frozenset((None, 'surrogate_or_replace',\\n29:                                       'surrogate_or_strict',\\n30:                                       'surrogate_then_replace'))\\n31: \\n32: \\n33: def to_bytes(obj, encoding='utf-8', errors=None, nonstring='simplerepr'):\\n34:     \\\"\\\"\\\"Make sure that a string is a byte string\\n35: \\n36:     :arg obj: An object to make sure is a byte string.  In most cases this\\n37:         will be either a text string or a byte string.  However, with\\n38:         ``nonstring='simplerepr'``, this can be used as a traceback-free\\n39:         version of ``str(obj)``.\\n40:     :kwarg encoding: The encoding to use to transform from a text string to\\n41:         a byte string.  Defaults to using 'utf-8'.\\n42:     :kwarg errors: The error handler to use if the text string is not\\n43:         encodable using the specified encoding.  Any valid `codecs error\\n44:         handler <https://docs.python.org/2/library/codecs.html#codec-base-classes>`_\\n45:         may be specified. There are three additional error strategies\\n46:         specifically aimed at helping people to port code.  The first two are:\\n47: \\n48:             :surrogate_or_strict: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\\n49:                 handler, otherwise it will use ``strict``\\n50:             :surrogate_or_replace: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\\n51:                 handler, otherwise it will use ``replace``.\\n52: \\n53:         Because ``surrogateescape`` was added in Python3 this usually means that\\n54:         Python3 will use ``surrogateescape`` and Python2 will use the fallback\\n55:         error handler. Note that the code checks for ``surrogateescape`` when the\\n56:         module is imported.  If you have a backport of ``surrogateescape`` for\\n57:         Python2, be sure to register the error handler prior to importing this\\n58:         module.\\n59: \\n60:         The last error handler is:\\n61: \\n62:             :surrogate_then_replace: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\\n63:                 handler.  If encoding with ``surrogateescape`` would traceback,\\n64:                 surrogates are first replaced with a replacement characters\\n65:                 and then the string is encoded using ``replace`` (which replaces\\n66:                 the rest of the nonencodable bytes).  If ``surrogateescape`` is\\n67:                 not present it will simply use ``replace``.  (Added in Ansible 2.3)\\n68:                 This strategy is designed to never traceback when it attempts\\n69:                 to encode a string.\\n70: \\n71:         The default until Ansible-2.2 was ``surrogate_or_replace``\\n72:         From Ansible-2.3 onwards, the default is ``surrogate_then_replace``.\\n73: \\n74:     :kwarg nonstring: The strategy to use if a nonstring is specified in\\n75:         ``obj``.  Default is 'simplerepr'.  Valid values are:\\n76: \\n77:         :simplerepr: The default.  This takes the ``str`` of the object and\\n78:             then returns the bytes version of that string.\\n79:         :empty: Return an empty byte string\\n80:         :passthru: Return the object passed in\\n81:         :strict: Raise a :exc:`TypeError`\\n82: \\n83:     :returns: Typically this returns a byte string.  If a nonstring object is\\n84:         passed in this may be a different type depending on the strategy\\n85:         specified by nonstring.  This will never return a text string.\\n86: \\n87:     .. note:: If passed a byte string, this function does not check that the\\n88:         string is valid in the specified encoding.  If it's important that the\\n89:         byte string is in the specified encoding do::\\n90: \\n91:             encoded_string = to_bytes(to_text(input_string, 'latin-1'), 'utf-8')\\n92: \\n93:     .. version_changed:: 2.3\\n94: \\n95:         Added the ``surrogate_then_replace`` error handler and made it the default error handler.\\n96:     \\\"\\\"\\\"\\n97:     if isinstance(obj, binary_type):\\n98:         return obj\\n99: \\n100:     # We're given a text string\\n101:     # If it has surrogates, we know because it will decode\\n102:     original_errors = errors\\n103:     if errors in _COMPOSED_ERROR_HANDLERS:\\n104:         if HAS_SURROGATEESCAPE:\\n105:             errors = 'surrogateescape'\\n106:         elif errors == 'surrogate_or_strict':\\n107:             errors = 'strict'\\n108:         else:\\n109:             errors = 'replace'\\n110: \\n111:     if isinstance(obj, text_type):\\n112:         try:\\n113:             # Try this first as it's the fastest\\n114:             return obj.encode(encoding, errors)\\n115:         except UnicodeEncodeError:\\n116:             if original_errors in (None, 'surrogate_then_replace'):\\n117:                 # However, with\n38:         ``nonstring='simplerepr'``, this can be used as a traceback-free\n39:         version of ``str(obj)``.\n40:     :kwarg encoding: The encoding to use to transform from a text string to\n41:         a byte string.  Defaults to using 'utf-8'.\n42:     :kwarg errors: The error handler to use if the text string is not\n43:         encodable using the specified encoding.  Any valid `codecs error\n44:         handler <https://docs.python.org/2/library/codecs.html#codec-base-classes>`_\n45:         may be specified. There are three additional error strategies\n46:         specifically aimed at helping people to port code.  The first two are:\n47: \n48:             :surrogate_or_strict: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\n49:                 handler, otherwise it will use ``strict``\n50:             :surrogate_or_replace: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\n51:                 handler, otherwise it will use ``replace``.\n52: \n53:         Because ``surrogateescape`` was added in Python3 this usually means that\n54:         Python3 will use ``surrogateescape`` and Python2 will use the fallback\n55:         error handler. Note that the code checks for ``surrogateescape`` when the\n56:         module is imported.  If you have a backport of ``surrogateescape`` for\n57:         Python2, be sure to register the error handler prior to importing this\n58:         module.\n59: \n60:         The last error handler is:\n61: \n62:             :surrogate_then_replace: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\n63:                 handler.  If encoding with ``surrogateescape`` would traceback,\n64:                 surrogates are first replaced with a replacement characters\n65:                 and then the string is encoded using ``replace`` (which replaces\n66:                 the rest of the nonencodable bytes).  If ``surrogateescape`` is\n67:                 not present it will simply use ``replace``.  (Added in Ansible 2.3)\n68:                 This strategy is designed to never traceback when it attempts\n69:                 to encode a string.\n70: \n71:         The default until Ansible-2.2 was ``surrogate_or_replace``\n72:         From Ansible-2.3 onwards, the default is ``surrogate_then_replace``.\n73: \n74:     :kwarg nonstring: The strategy to use if a nonstring is specified in\n75:         ``obj``.  Default is 'simplerepr'.  Valid values are:\n76: \n77:         :simplerepr: The default.  This takes the ``str`` of the object and\n78:             then returns the bytes version of that string.\n79:         :empty: Return an empty byte string\n80:         :passthru: Return the object passed in\n81:         :strict: Raise a :exc:`TypeError`\n82: \n83:     :returns: Typically this returns a byte string.  If a nonstring object is\n84:         passed in this may be a different type depending on the strategy\n85:         specified by nonstring.  This will never return a text string.\n86: \n87:     .. note:: If passed a byte string, this function does not check that the\n88:         string is valid in the specified encoding.  If it's important that the\n89:         byte string is in the specified encoding do::\n90: \n91:             encoded_string = to_bytes(to_text(input_string, 'latin-1'), 'utf-8')\n92: \n93:     .. version_changed:: 2.3\n94: \n95:         Added the ``surrogate_then_replace`` error handler and made it the default error handler.\n96:     \"\"\"\n97:     if isinstance(obj, binary_type):\n98:         return obj\n99: \n100:     # In most cases this\n        will be either a text string or a byte string.  However, with\n        ``nonstring='simplerepr'``, this can be used as a traceback-free\n        version of ``str(obj)``.\n    :kwarg encoding: The encoding to use to transform from a text string to\n        a byte string. Any valid `codecs error\n        handler <https://docs.python.org/2/library/codecs.html#codec-base-classes>`_\n        may be specified. There are three additional error strategies\n        specifically aimed at helping people to port code.  The first two are:\n\n            :surrogate_or_strict: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\n                handler, otherwise it will use ``strict``\n            :surrogate_or_replace: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\n                handler, otherwise it will use ``replace``.\n\n        Because ``surrogateescape`` was added in Python3 this usually means that\n        Python3 will use ``surrogateescape`` and Python2 will use the fallback\n        error handler. Note that the code checks for ``surrogateescape`` when the\n        module is imported.  If you have a backport of ``surrogateescape`` for\n        Python2, be sure to register the error handler prior to importing this\n        module.\n\n        The last error handler is:\n\n            :surrogate_then_replace: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\n                handler.  If encoding with ``surrogateescape`` would traceback,\n                surrogates are first replaced with a replacement characters\n                and then the string is encoded using ``replace`` (which replaces\n                the rest of the nonencodable bytes).  If ``surrogateescape`` is\n                not present it will simply use ``replace``. Default is 'simplerepr'.  Valid values are:\n\n        :simplerepr: The default.  This takes the ``str`` of the object and\n            then returns the bytes version of that string.\n        :empty: Return an empty byte string\n        :passthru: Return the object passed in\n        :strict: Raise a :exc:`TypeError`\n\n    :returns: Typically this returns a byte string.  If a nonstring object is\n        passed in this may be a different type depending on the strategy\n        specified by nonstring. If you have a backport of ``surrogateescape`` for\n        Python2, be sure to register the error handler prior to importing this\n        module.\n\n        The last error handler is:\n\n            :surrogate_then_replace: Will use ``surrogateescape`` if it is a valid\n                handler.  If encoding with ``surrogateescape`` would traceback,\n                surrogates are first replaced with a replacement characters\n                and then the string is encoded using ``replace`` (which replaces\n                the rest of the nonencodable bytes).  If ``surrogateescape`` is\n                not present it will simply use ``replace``.  (Added in Ansible 2.3)\n                This strategy is designed to never traceback when it attempts\n                to encode a string.\n\n        The default until Ansible-2.2 was ``surrogate_or_replace``\n        From Ansible-2.3 onwards, the default is ``surrogate_then_replace``.\n\n    :kwarg nonstring: The strategy to use if a nonstring is specified in\n        ``obj``.  Default is 'simplerepr'.  Valid values are:\n\n        :simplerepr: The default.  This takes the ``str`` of the object and\n            then returns the bytes version of that string.\n        :empty: Return an empty byte string\n        :passthru: Return the object passed in\n        :strict: Raise a :exc:`TypeError`\n\n    :returns: Typically this returns a byte string.  If a nonstring object is\n        passed in this may be a different type depending on the strategy\n        specified by nonstring.  This will never return a text string.\n\n    .. note:: If passed a byte string, this function does not check that the\n        string is valid in the specified encoding.  If it's important that the\n        byte string is in the specified encoding do::\n\n            encoded_string = to_bytes(to_text(input_string, 'latin-1'), 'utf-8')\n\n    .. version_changed:: 2.3\n\n        Added the ``surrogate_then_replace`` error handler and made it the default error handler.\n    \"\"\"\n    if isinstance(obj, binary_type):\n        return obj\n\n    # We're given a text string\n    # If it has surrogates, we know because it will decode\n    original_errors = errors\n    if errors in _COMPOSED_ERROR_HANDLERS:\n        if HAS_SURROGATEESCAPE:\n            errors = 'surrogateescape'\n        elif errors == 'surrogate_or_strict':\n            errors = 'strict'\n        else:\n            errors = 'replace'\n\n    if isinstance(obj, text_type):\n        try:\n            # Try this first as it's the fastest\n            return obj.encode(encoding, errors)\n        except UnicodeEncodeError:\n            if original_errors in (None, 'surrogate_then_replace'):\n                # We should only reach this if encoding was non-utf8 original_errors was\n                # surrogate_then_escape and errors was surrogateescape\n\n                # Slow but works\n                return_string = obj.encode('utf-8', 'surrogateescape')\n                return_string = return_string.decode('utf-8', 'replace')\n                return return_string.encode(encoding, 'replace')\n            raise\n\n    # Note: We do these last even though we have to call to_bytes again on the\n    # value because we're optimizing the common case\n    if nonstring == 'simplerepr':\n        try:\n            value = str(obj)\n        except UnicodeError:\n            try:\n                value = repr(obj)\n            except UnicodeError:\n                # Giving up\n                return to_bytes('')\n    elif nonstring == 'passthru':\n        return obj\n    elif nonstring == 'empty':\n        # python2.4 doesn't have b''\n        return to_bytes('')\n    elif nonstring == 'strict':\n        raise TypeError('obj must be a string type')\n    else:\n        raise TypeError('Invalid value %s for to_bytes\\' nonstring parameter' % nonstring)\n\n    return to_bytes(value, encoding, errors)\n\n\ndef to_text(obj, encoding='utf-8', errors=None, nonstring='simplerepr'):\n    \"\"\"Make sure that a string is a text string\n\n    :arg obj: An object to make sure is a text string.  In most cases this\n        will be either a text string or a byte string.  However, with\n        ``nonstring='simplerepr'``, this can be used as a traceback-free\n        version of ``str(obj)``.\n    :kwarg encoding: The encoding to use to transform from a byte string to\n        a text string.  Defaults to using 'utf-8'.\n    :kwarg errors: The error handler to use if the byte string is not\n        decodable using the specified encoding.  Any valid `codecs error\n        handler <https://docs.python.org/2/library/codecs.html#codec-base-classes>`_\n        may be specified.   We support three additional error strategies\n        specifically aimed at helping people to port code:\n\n            :surrogate_or_strict: Will use surrogateescape if it is a valid\n                handler, otherwise it will use strict\n            :surrogate_or_replace: Will use surrogateescape if it is a valid\n                handler, otherwise it will use replace.\n            :surrogate_then_replace: Does the same as surrogate_or_replace but\n                `was added for symmetry with the error handlers in\n                :func:`ansible.module_utils._text.to_bytes` (Added in Ansible 2.3)\n\n        Because surrogateescape was added in Python3 this usually means that\n        Python3 will use `surrogateescape` and Python2 will use the fallback\n        error handler. Note that the code checks for surrogateescape when the\n        module is imported.  If you have a backport of `surrogateescape` for\n        python2, be sure to register the error handler prior to importing this\n        module.\n\n        The default until Ansible-2.2 was `surrogate_or_replace`\n        In Ansible-2.3 this defaults to `surrogate_then_replace` for symmetry\n        with :func:`ansible.module_utils._text.to_bytes` .\n    :kwarg nonstring: The strategy to use if a nonstring is specified in\n        ``obj``.  Default is 'simplerepr'.  Valid values are:\n\n        :simplerepr: The default.  This takes the ``str`` of the object and\n            then returns the text version of that string.\n        :empty: Return an empty text string\n        :passthru: Return the object passed in\n        :strict: Raise a :exc:`TypeError`\n\n    :returns: Typically this returns a text string.  If a nonstring object is\n        passed in this may be a different type depending on the strategy\n        specified by nonstring.  This will never return a byte string.\n        From Ansible-2.3 onwards, the default is `surrogate_then_replace`.\n\n    .. version_changed:: 2.3\n\n        Added the surrogate_then_replace error handler and made it the default error handler.\n    \"\"\"\n    if isinstance(obj, text_type):\n        return obj\n\n    if errors in _COMPOSED_ERROR_HANDLERS:\n        if HAS_SURROGATEESCAPE:\n            errors = 'surrogateescape'\n        elif errors == 'surrogate_or_strict':\n            errors = 'strict'\n        else:\n            errors = 'replace'\n\n    if isinstance(obj, binary_type):\n        # Note: We don't need special handling for surrogate_then_replace\n        # because all bytes will either be made into surrogates or are valid\n        # to decode.\n        return obj.decode(encoding, errors)\n\n    # Note: We do these last even though we have to call to_text again on the\n    # value because we're optimizing the common case\n    if nonstring == 'simplerepr':\n        try:\n            value = str(obj)\n        except UnicodeError:\n            try:\n                value = repr(obj)\n            except UnicodeError:\n                # Giving up\n                return u''\n    elif nonstring == 'passthru':\n        return obj\n    elif nonstring == 'empty':\n        return u''\n    elif nonstring == 'strict':\n        raise TypeError('obj must be a string type')\n    else:\n        raise TypeError('Invalid value %s for to_text\\'s nonstring parameter' % nonstring)\n\n    return to_text(value, encoding, errors)\n\n\n#: :py:func:`to_native`\n#:      Transform a variable into the native str type for the python version\n#:\n#:      On Python2, this is an alias for\n#:      :func:`~ansible.module_utils.to_bytes`.  On Python3 it is an alias for\n#:      :func:`~ansible.module_utils.to_text`.  It makes it easier to\n#:      transform a variable into the native str type for the python version\n#:      the code is running on.  Use this when constructing the message to\n#:      send to exceptions or when dealing with an API that needs to take\n#:      a native string.  Example::\n#:\n#:          try:\n#:              1//0\n#:          except ZeroDivisionError as e:\n#:              raise MyException('Encountered and error: %s' % to_native(e))\nif PY3:\n    to_native = to_text\nelse:\n    to_native = to_bytes\n\n\ndef _json_encode_fallback(obj):\n    if isinstance(obj, Set):\n        return list(obj)\n    elif isinstance(obj, datetime.datetime):\n        return obj.isoformat()\n    raise TypeError(\"Cannot json serialize %s\" % to_native(obj))\n\n\ndef jsonify(data, **kwargs):\n    for encoding in (\"utf-8\", \"latin-1\"):\n        try:\n            return json.dumps(data, encoding=encoding, default=_json_encode_fallback, **kwargs)\n        # Old systems using old simplejson module does not support encoding keyword.\n        except TypeError:\n            try:\n                new_data = container_to_text(data, encoding=encoding)\n            except UnicodeDecodeError:\n                continue\n            return json.dumps(new_data, default=_json_encode_fallback, **kwargs)\n        except UnicodeDecodeError:\n            continue\n    raise UnicodeError('Invalid unicode encoding encountered')\n\n\ndef container_to_bytes(d, encoding='utf-8', errors='surrogate_or_strict'):\n    ''' Recursively convert dict keys and values to byte str\n\n        Specialized for json return because this only handles, lists, tuples,\n        and dict container types (the containers that the json module returns)\n    '''\n\n    if isinstance(d, text_type):\n        return to_bytes(d, encoding=encoding, errors=errors)\n    elif isinstance(d, dict):\n        return dict(container_to_bytes(o, encoding, errors) for o in iteritems(d))\n    elif isinstance(d, list):\n        return [container_to_bytes(o, encoding, errors) for o in d]\n    elif isinstance(d, tuple):\n        return tuple(container_to_bytes(o, encoding, errors) for o in d)\n    else:\n        return d\n\n\ndef container_to_text(d, encoding='utf-8', errors='surrogate_or_strict'):\n    \"\"\"Recursively convert dict keys and values to text str\n\n    Specialized for json return because this only handles, lists, tuples,\n    and dict container types (the containers that the json module returns)\n    \"\"\"\n\n    if isinstance(d, binary_type):\n        # The respawned process inherits only\n    26→    stdout/stderr from the current process.\n    27→\n    28→    Only a single respawn is allowed. ``respawn_module`` will fail on nested respawns.
